# 1200\n
{Cambridge, Trinity Homilies, Hand A: (all texts in this hand apart from Poema Morale tagged under this number - 1200)}\n
C12b2\n
552 238 W\n
{=NW Essex=}\n
{=Sample represents all the English in this hand apart from the Poema\n
Morale which is in a somewhat different kind of language, also from\n
Essex, for which see tagged text no 4. Hand A writes pp. 1-24, 26 (three\n
words only), 27, 53 line 15-end, 58 line 6-end, 114-118, 121-123,\n
128-133, 137, 139, 142-143 first 5 words, 151 word 8-end=}\n
{=Script - late twelfth-century book hand. A mixed script with elements\n
of proto-Gothic and also English Caroline minuscule=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The Trinity Homilies follow straight on from the Poema Morale in the\n
same hand but in a somewhat different type of language. The manuscript,\n
having been foliated for the first gathering is now paginated beginning\n
at 1 (the equivalent of fol. 10r). Each homily is here given a different\n
text number which appears in braces at its head. All the homilies in\n
Hand A are in the same kind of language amalgamated for mapping as text\n
no. 1200. An early C16 secretary hand provides glosses, as noted below.\n
It is the same as that which glossed three words in the Poema Morale.\n
The glossed words are usually underlined by the glossing scribe and\n
the glosses interlined above, or in the margin when the word is near\n
the edge of the text block. Sometimes words are underlined, whether\n
by this scribe or some other, without being glossed. The later scribe\n
responsible for the underlining also puts marking sigla of various kinds\n
in the margins. For a full bibliographical description of the\n
manuscript, see Betty Hill, \'Trinity College Cambridge MS. B. 14. 52,\n
and William Patten\', Transactions of the Cambridge Bibliographical\n
Society 4 (1966), 192-200 and \'Cambridge, Trinity College MS B.14.52\',\n
TCBS 12 (2003), 393-402. Here, in the transcription of the Latin\n
text, punctus and punctus elevatus used as punctuation are separated\n
from the words preceding and following them. Punctus used to indicate\n
abbreviation are here placed immediately after the expansion of the\n
abbreviation, to differentiate this function=}\n
{21}\n

{=Morris (1873) prints O[N] HELLE as though N has been omitted and H\n
written clearly. But there is no space after O and there is a space\n
immediately before ELLE. The letter after O is not H: the ascender\n
lacks any sign of a forked top and the \'limb\' is not drawn down below\n
the baseline to the left as is regular with Scribe A\'s H, but looks\n
like the normal \'foot\' of a minim.  I believe the letter is N, perhaps\n
corrected from the beginnings of an F which would account for the\n
rather taller than usual first element=}\n
